Source: Architectural Analysis of Telemetry Signals: Engineering the MELTS Observability FrameworkType: Pasted TextArchitectural Analysis of Telemetry Signals: Engineering the MELTS Observability FrameworkDistributed, cloud-native software architectures require a shift from legacy system monitoring to advanced telemetry engineering.[1, 2] Traditional monitoring techniques typically focus on tracking known system failure states, functioning as a reactive insurance policy.[1, 3] Modern observability is proactive, designed to explore complex, unknown execution patterns and reconstruct end-to-end paths across arbitrary process and network boundaries.[1, 4]Telemetry Signals (MELTS): Evolution and Structural StandardizationThe technical foundation of this capability is the MELTS framework, representing five core classes of telemetry signals: Metrics, Events, Logs, Traces, and Continuous Profiling.[1, 3] Reconciling these signals across modern infrastructures reveals that different paradigms define and utilize these data types according to specialized database and operational needs.[1, 3, 5]Acronym Variations and the Transition to Operational IntelligenceThe core of observability has historically rested on the three pillars of metrics, logs, and traces, but the addition of events formalized the MELT framework.[3]The Event Axis (MELT): Events capture unusual state changes, threshold breaches, or key lifecycle transitions, acting as a crucial bridge between raw metrics and detailed execution logs.[1]The SQL Database Axis (M.E.L.T.S): In modernized enterprise architectures and hybrid cloud systems, Oracle Database Administrators and system engineers integrate SQL execution statistics as a distinct fifth telemetry class.[5] This M.E.L.T.S configuration combines Metrics, Events, Logs, Traces, and SQL to leverage database instrumentation directly within cloud-native application modernization projects.[5]The Profiling Axis (MELTS / The Fifth Pillar): In the broader cloud-native landscape, continuous profiling is recognized as the fifth pillar.[1] It maps real-time CPU, memory, and runtime resource consumption directly to specific lines of code without requiring manual code changes.[1, 6]This comprehensive telemetry framework supports "Operational Intelligence," which translates technical runtime data into actionable business value.[3] For example, tracing data helps product managers analyze new feature adoption rates and customer retention.[3]At the same time, system logs and traces feed security scanning tools to track code vulnerabilities, verify supply chain health, and monitor resolution times.[3] This treats observability as a strategic investment that drives competitive advantage, rather than a simple cost center.[3]OpenTelemetry Standardization and Package ArchitectureTo prevent the fragmentation of telemetry data across proprietary monitoring tools, the Cloud Native Computing Foundation (CNCF) hosts OpenTelemetry (OTel).[1, 2] As an open-source, vendor-neutral framework, OTel standardizes the generation, collection, and export of traces, metrics, and logs.[1, 2]This allows organizations to process legacy, proprietary telemetry formats into OTel-compliant structures using data modernization services (such as the Cloud Fabrix Observability Data Modernization Service, or ODMS).[2] These standardized signals are then ingested by an OpenTelemetry Collector at the edge or within a multi-cloud architecture and routed to high-performance backends.[1, 2]To ensure consistent implementation across programming languages, every signal in the OpenTelemetry project is divided into four standardized architectural packages [1]:The API Package: Public-facing interfaces used directly in application code for instrumentation.[1] They establish code boundaries and facilitate data generation without binding the application to a specific vendor SDK.[1, 8]The SDK Package: The concrete, language-specific implementation of the API.[1] It manages the operational pipelines of the telemetry framework, controlling in-process data aggregation, memory buffering, sampling strategies, and transport export protocols.[1, 9]Semantic Conventions: A standardized registry of key-value attributes that define the naming and meaning of resources, database actions, and system metadata across all telemetry signals.[1, 10]The Contrib Package: Community-maintained plugins and third-party integrations that extend the core SDK.[1] This package enables autoinstrumentation for frameworks, database drivers, and messaging brokers.[1, 8]Metrics: Engineering Performance AggregationsMetrics provide numerical representations of system state and behavioral trends captured over consistent time intervals.[1, 9] The OpenTelemetry metrics pipeline operates through three distinct architectural layers: the API layer, which records inline application measurements; the SDK layer, which aggregates these raw measurements into compact records; and the Exporter layer, which serializes and transmits the data to a storage backend.[9]Cumulative and Delta CountersCounters track the occurrence rate and volume of system events.[9, 11]Cumulative Counters: These instruments maintain a strictly increasing total that only resets to zero when the application restarts.[11, 12] The client application stores the running total, making this approach highly resilient to transient network drops.[11, 12]Delta Counters: These instruments track event counts only since the last metric flush.[12] The client emits only the local delta and resets its internal counter to zero.[12] While this reduces memory usage on the client, it makes the system more sensitive to data loss if an export batch fails.[12]UpDownCounters and Fluctuating ResourcesWhen tracking resources that fluctuate over time, systems use the UpDownCounter.[9] Unlike standard counters, UpDownCounters are non-monotonic, allowing inline addition and subtraction of positive or negative values.[9] This instrument is ideal for tracking active system states, such as active HTTP request connections, background thread counts, cache allocations, or database connection pool utilization.[9, 13]Gauges and Asynchronous Instrument PatternsA Gauge captures an instantaneous, non-additive measurement at a specific point in time.[9, 11]Synchronous Gauges: These record fluctuating values immediately as they occur inline with application logic.[9] This is ideal for capturing values that change due to direct code execution, such as reporting a database transaction execution time or a configuration parameter change.[9]Observable Gauges (Asynchronous): These instruments use a callback-driven approach.[9] Instead of recording inline during application execution, the SDK queries a registered callback function at predefined collection intervals.[9] This pattern is highly efficient for system-level metrics that do not depend on specific application events, such as tracking system memory footprint or CPU core utilization.[9]Histograms and Dynamic Distribution MechanicsHistograms record the distribution of values by classifying individual observations into distinct ranges or buckets.[11, 12] Rather than tracking a single cumulative total, the histogram SDK aggregates observations to output statistical details like sum, count, minimum, maximum, and individual bucket counts.[9] This structure is critical for latency and payload size analysis, where single mathematical averages can obscure severe tail-end anomalies.[9, 13]Classic Histograms: These require static bucket boundaries defined upfront.[11, 14] This can lead to low resolution if the data shifts, or high memory usage if too many buckets are configured to capture tail-end latency.[11, 14]Native or Exponential Histograms: These use a dynamic bucketing schema.[11, 12] This ensures that time series remain fully aggregatable across different system instances and network transfers, maintaining high statistical resolution.[11, 12] For example, when a metric backend (such as Chronosphere) detects that a metric stream has exceeded a defined bucket limit (like 160 buckets), it automatically doubles the bucket width to consolidate the data into fewer, wider buckets.[12] This maintains the metric's aggregatability without exceeding memory limits.[12]Summaries and Client-Side QuantilesSimilar to histograms, Summaries track statistical distributions, but they calculate quantiles (like p50, p90, or p99) on the client side using a sliding time window.[11, 13] This provides immediate quantile data without requiring backend calculations.[13, 14]However, because these quantiles are pre-calculated on individual application instances, they cannot be aggregated across different nodes or microservice instances.[13] This limitation makes summaries less suitable for global cluster analysis, where native histograms are preferred.[11, 13]Raw MeasurementsA Measurement represents a single, raw observation with a discrete, arbitrary value.[12] Unlike counters or gauges, measurements do not represent running totals or continuous states; instead, they capture individual data points that can be aggregated later using various rollup rules.[12]A major operational risk in metric systems is high cardinality.[14] Appending high-cardinality attributes—such as unique user IDs, IP addresses, or transaction hashes—to metrics creates thousands of unique time-series variations.[14] This can quickly degrade backend storage and slow down query execution.[14]Logs: Structural Evolution and Contextual EventsLogs provide a timestamped history of discrete execution events.[15] While logs have a long history in software systems, their operational utility in production is heavily dependent on how they are structured.[15, 16]The Structural Spectrum of LoggingUnstructured Logs: These logs consist of free-form, plain-text strings.[15] Although highly human-readable and simple to write during initial development, unstructured logs are difficult for machines to parse at scale.[15, 16] Standardizing and filtering them in production requires intensive, fragile regular expression parsing that can quickly strain log-management systems.[15, 16]Semistructured Logs: These logs capture key-value pairs or delimited text strings, but they lack a consistent schema across different services.[15, 17] While they are easier to parse than unstructured logs, their inconsistent formatting across services makes it difficult to standardize and analyze them reliably.[15]Structured Logs: These logs use a standardized, machine-readable format, such as JSON or Protobuf, built on a stable and consistent schema.[15, 16] What makes a log structured is the presence of this stable schema with well-defined typed fields, not merely that it is valid JSON.[15] Structured logging allows log-management platforms to directly parse and index key-value fields (like client IPs, error codes, and request durations) without resource-intensive regex parsing.[16, 18] This enables fast searching, complex analytics, and reliable alerting.[16, 18]Events as High-Context LogsWithin modern telemetry, all events are recorded as log structures, but not all logs qualify as events.[15] An event is a specialized log that denotes an unusual change or threshold breach in a system, such as a failed login attempt or a database connection timeout.[1, 16] Events carry rich, structured metadata to provide immediate context about the "who, where, and how" of system state changes.[1, 16]OpenTelemetry Log Record Layout and Logging BridgesOpenTelemetry provides a unified API and SDK designed to capture log data, using a standardized log record layout.[15] This layout defines concrete top-level fields alongside flexible resource attributes [15]:Timestamp: The precise UTC time when the event occurred within the application thread.[15]ObservedTimestamp: The UTC time when the log record was received and processed by the logging SDK or collector.[15] This is critical for diagnosing network latency bottlenecks or ingestion delays.[15]TraceId and SpanId: Standardized fields that correlate the log directly with an active execution span, linking granular logs to end-to-end request traces.[4, 15]SeverityText and SeverityNumber: Fields that map various language-specific logging levels (like INFO, DEBUG, or CRITICAL) to a standardized range for consistent filtering.[15]Body: The primary message string or structured log payload.[15]Resource and Attributes: Flexible key-value pairs that capture environmental metadata, such as Kubernetes container names, cloud providers, and deployment versions.[15, 18]Rather than requiring developers to rewrite all legacy application logs, OpenTelemetry uses "logging bridges".[15] These bridges intercept logs from standard logging frameworks (such as Log4j, Winston, or Logback) and convert them directly into OTel-compliant structures.[15]SIEM Integration and Threat HuntingStandardized, structured logging is critical for modern Security Information and Event Management (SIEM) platforms.[16] Because structured logs use a consistent schema, SIEM tools can parse and cross-reference events in real time without being slowed down by regex parsing.[16]This allows security teams to build accurate correlation rules across multiple sources (like firewalls, Kubernetes clusters, and cloud services).[16] For example, security tools can easily trace brute-force login attempts or track lateral movement by searching for a specific IP address across all indexed services, helping to reduce alert fatigue.[16]Traces: Pathway Contextualization and Distributed JourneysDistributed tracing maps request pathways as they cross network and service boundaries, highlighting processing bottlenecks and failures.[19, 20] A complete trace is made up of individual operations called spans.[1, 19] Every span represents a specific unit of work, and they are organized hierarchically starting from a single root span.[19, 21]Context Propagation and W3C StandardsContext propagation is the mechanism that carries trace metadata across service boundaries.[4, 19] This process serializes trace metadata on the sending side, injects it into network carriers (like HTTP headers), and extracts it on the receiving side to link child spans back to their parents.[4, 20]According to the W3C Trace Context specification, this metadata is standardized across two primary headers [4, 20]:traceparent: A compact, hyphen-delimited string that acts as the primary trace carrier (e.g., 00-0af7651916cd43dd8448eb211c80319c-b7ad6b7169203331-01).[20] It contains four distinct components [20]:Version: A 2-hex-digit version indicator, currently set to 00.[20]Trace ID: A 32-character lowercase hex string that uniquely identifies the entire transaction.[20]Parent Span ID: A 16-character lowercase hex string identifying the active caller span.[20]Trace Flags: A 2-character hex flag conveying sampling decisions (where 01 denotes that the trace has been sampled).[20]tracestate: A comma-separated list of key-value pairs (e.g., vendor1=value1,vendor2=value2) designed to carry vendor-specific routing and filtering metadata without breaking trace interoperability across different tools.[20]Additionally, Baggage enables the propagation of arbitrary key-value pairs across service boundaries.[4, 21] Because baggage context travels end-to-end, engineers must prevent PII leakage by sanitizing or blocking sensitive data before passing context to external services.[4, 19]Context Propagation in Event Mesh BrokersTracing requests in asynchronous, event-driven architectures introduces unique challenges for event brokers (such as Solace or AMQP message queues).[19, 21] When a message broker receives an event, it performs several operational checks, such as verifying access controls and checking if the target queue is full.[19, 21]If these checks succeed and the message is traced, the broker extracts the incoming trace context, creates a child span representing the queue operation, and injects the updated context into the message headers before persistence.[19, 21] If the broker is overloaded or its internal telemetry queues are full, it passes the original, unmodified trace context unchanged to the next downstream consumer.[19, 21] This ensures trace continuity even when telemetry resources are constrained.[19, 21]Tracing in Synchronous versus Asynchronous SystemsIn synchronous, request-response systems (like direct HTTP APIs), tracing typically uses a parent-child model.[22] Each child span contains a direct reference to the parent span that triggered it, creating a single hierarchical tree that is easy to view in waterfall-style timelines.[22] This approach is ideal for tightly coupled, synchronous execution paths where spans happen quickly and sequentially.[22]Asynchronous architectures (like event-driven systems using Kafka, RabbitMQ, or background task workers) introduce temporal and thread decoupling.[22] Because messages might sit in queues or be processed in parallel across different threads, forcing a strict parent-child relationship can result in fragmented or hard-to-read traces.[22]To handle this, systems use span-link tracing.[22] Instead of forcing a single hierarchical trace, receiving services start a new trace and use a span link to logically connect back to the original trace ID passed in the message payload.[22] This approach handles fan-in workflows (where one operation depends on multiple incoming traces) and long-running, multi-stage pipelines without cluttering individual trace timelines.[22]Continuous Profiling: Kernel-Level Resource ContextContinuous profiling provides line-of-code visibility into production resource consumption.[6] The standardization of profiling advanced with the graduation of the OpenTelemetry Profiles signal to Alpha, driven by Elastic's donation of its eBPF profiling agent.[7, 10]The Mechanics of eBPF ProfilingTraditional profilers required runtime hooks, manual code instrumentation, or service restarts, which often introduced significant CPU overhead.[7, 23] In contrast, eBPF (Extended Berkeley Packet Filter) profiling agents run directly within the Linux kernel.[7, 10]This allows the agent to observe all running processes, language runtimes, compiled binaries, and third-party libraries without modifying the application code.[7, 10] The eBPF agent captures stack traces by sampling CPU instruction pointers at regular intervals.[6]To resolve these pointers into human-readable function names, the system uses "symbolization," which maps compiled addresses back to their source code locations.[7, 10] To optimize data transfers, the OpenTelemetry Profiles protocol uses a deduplicated stack representation, storing each unique call stack once in dictionary tables.[10]Process Context SharingA major challenge for kernel-level profiling is associating raw OS process IDs with application metadata (such as service name, version, and environment).[7] To solve this, OpenTelemetry developed the Process Context Sharing OTEP (OpenTelemetry Enhancement Proposal).[7]This mechanism allows application SDKs to publish their active resource attributes into a standardized memory region.[7] The eBPF profiler reads this memory region to automatically enrich stack traces with application-level metadata, bridging the gap between kernel-level profiling and application context.[7]Overhead Limits and Tuning ParametersSampling profilers limit resource impact by capturing system state at a specific frequency (typically a timer-based loop running at f = 100\text{ Hz}).[6]\text{Sampling Frequency } (f) = 100\text{ Hz} \implies \Delta t = 10\text{ ms}\text{Aggregated Flush Window } (\tau) = 15\text{ s}Instead of streaming every sample individually, the profiling agent aggregates stack traces in memory and flushes them to the backend in batches every 15\text{ seconds}.[6] This approach keeps CPU overhead within a low single-digit range (typically 1% to 3%) and maintains a very small memory footprint (typically under 50\text{ MB} per application instance).[6]To safe-guard systems during traffic spikes, architectures can implement several optimization strategies [23, 24]:Adaptive Sampling: This strategy dynamically scales the profiling sample rate based on real-time system load.[24] Under standard operating conditions, the profiler runs at its default frequency.[24] If system CPU or memory usage crosses a defined safety threshold, the sampler dynamically lowers its rate to protect application performance.[23, 24]Canary Validation Process: Before enabling profiling across an entire fleet, teams deploy the profiling agent to a single "canary" instance running under stable production traffic.[6] By comparing the canary's performance metrics (like request throughput, error rates, and p95/p99 latencies) against unprofiled instances, team can safely measure the profiler's real-world impact.[6]Analyzing Profiler CPU Flame Graphs: Because profiling agents are self-profiling, engineers can inspect the generated flame graphs to identify frames associated with the profiler itself (e.g., async-profiler or native kernel timing loops).[6] The percentage of samples containing these frames directly corresponds to the CPU overhead introduced by the tool.[6]Certain workloads remain challenging to profile, including very short-lived sub-millisecond tasks, high-churn network connections, and highly optimized native code (such as vectorized database kernels).[6] These workloads can increase sampling overhead and require custom profiling adjustments.[6]Telemetry Analytics, Performance, and Database EconomicsManaging telemetry at scale introduces significant storage and ingestion challenges.[25, 26] High-volume systems can ingest hundreds of terabytes or petabytes of data, meaning storage costs often exceed compute costs.[26]At this scale, query performance is heavily limited by storage read bandwidth, as the active dataset far exceeds system RAM and operating system page cache capacity.[26] To address this, modern observability platforms rely on two primary architectural optimizations [26]:Aggressive Data Compression: Telemetry data, especially structured logs and traces, contains highly repetitive fields.[26] Implementing columnar storage and specialized compression algorithms regularly achieves a 10\times reduction in disk footprint, transforming a query over 100\text{ TB} from an impractical 2.7-hour operation into a fast, compressed read.[26]Column-Oriented Storage: Unlike row-oriented stores, columnar databases isolate data fields into separate physical streams on disk.[26] Queries analyzing request durations across billions of transactions only read the relevant duration column, minimizing I/O bottlenecks.[26]\text{Query Latency } (t_{\text{query}}) = \frac{D}{B}Where D represents the total active telemetry volume read from disk, and B represents the sustained read bandwidth of the storage subsystem.[26]For a raw telemetry volume of 100\text{ TB} (10^5\text{ GB}) processed over a storage layout offering a sustained read bandwidth of 10\text{ GB/s}:t_{\text{raw}} = \frac{100,000\text{ GB}}{10\text{ GB/s}} = 10,000\text{ seconds} \approx 2.78\text{ hours}Applying a standard 10\times columnar compression algorithm reduces the physical on-disk size to 10\text{ TB}, yielding:t_{\text{compressed}} = \frac{10,000\text{ GB}}{10\text{ GB/s}} = 1,000\text{ seconds} \approx 16.67\text{ minutes}If the storage engine is column-oriented, a query evaluating a single metric (e.g., an 8-byte float representing request duration) over 100\text{ billion} logs only reads the isolated column data, resulting in a raw column volume (V_{\text{col}}) of:V_{\text{col}} = 100 \times 10^9 \times 8\text{ bytes} = 800\text{ GB}Reading this isolated column directly from disk requires:t_{\text{columnar}} = \frac{800\text{ GB}}{10\text{ GB/s}} = 80\text{ seconds}This shows how columnar databases optimize query performance over large volumes.[26]High-Dimensional Vector Similarity SearchModern telemetry analytics use high-dimensional vectors to search and correlate profiles and metrics.[27] Approximate Nearest Neighbor Search (ANNS) schemes, such as Hierarchical Navigable Small World (HNSW) graphs and DiskANN, provide fast similarity searches across large-scale datasets.[27]However, because these graph indices and their raw data must reside in memory to maintain accuracy, they require significant memory footprints—often 300\text{ GB} to 700\text{ GB} for billion-scale datasets.[27] This random traversal pattern across graph nodes can also lead to high CPU cache miss rates (typically 80% to 95%), requiring careful memory optimizations.[27]Traffic Modeling and Latency AnalysisTo manage heavy-tailed distributions and high-dimensional categorical fields (like port-protocol pairs) in telemetry streams, advanced platforms (such as PRVTEL) use specialized encoding techniques [28]:Log-Transformations: Prior to modeling, a logarithmic transformation compress the dynamic range of skewed traffic data, such as packet and byte counts.[28]Bayesian Gaussian Mixture Models (GMMs): GMMs model these compressed, heavy-tailed distributions as a weighted sum of Gaussian components, capturing complex system variations.[28]Word2Vec Embeddings: Categorical network fields (such as source IP, port, and protocol combinations) are mapped into dense vector embeddings.[28] This reduces dimensionality compared to one-hot encoding, allowing analytics engines to identify similar traffic patterns across different services.[28]Agent-Level Persistence AlternativesFor edge collectors or resource-constrained agent architectures, choosing the right local storage engine is critical to managing memory and write throughput.[29]Synthesis and Strategic RecommendationsObservability is not merely a collection of tools; it is an organizational practice that translates telemetry into continuous operational improvements.[3] To extract maximum value from telemetry data while keeping performance and storage costs under control, organizations should implement the following strategic patterns:Standardize on OpenTelemetry APIs and SDKs: Implement vendor-agnostic instrumentation across all applications to avoid vendor lock-in, simplify data routing, and guarantee data ownership.[1, 2]Enforce Structured, Schema-Validated Logging: Transition away from unstructured logs to structured JSON logging.[15, 16] Establish a shared, stable schema across all engineering teams to enable efficient SIEM parsing, fast searching, and automated alerting.[16, 18]Deploy eBPF-Based Continuous Profiling: Introduce kernel-level, low-overhead continuous profiling to identify code-level bottlenecks.[7, 10] Use adaptive sampling configurations to protect production workloads during sudden traffic surges.[23, 24]Optimize Storage Budgets with Columnar Engines: Consolidate high-volume logging and tracing pipelines onto columnar databases like ClickHouse to achieve high data compression, reduce storage footprints, and maintain fast query performance.[25, 26]Establish Service Level Objectives (SLOs): Rather than alerting on every minor metric fluctuation, focus on business-oriented SLOs that track user-facing impacts to reduce alert fatigue and align engineering efforts with business priorities.[3]A starter's guide: Observability with OpenTelemetry | New Relic, Suite, Advanced Event Mesh Releases Get Support Creating and Managing Event Broker Services / Configuring and Managing Messaging / Enabling Distributed Tracing / Distributed Tracing Overview / Context Propagation for Distributed Tracing On this page Context Propagation in the Event Broker Context Propagation with Solace Messaging APIs Visualizing Trace Information in the Backend Context Propagation for Distributed Tracing As discussed in the overview, distributed tracing tracks an event all the way from the publishing application, between event brokers, and to the receiving application. A distributed trace, or simply a trace, is a record of the path taken by an event message as it propagates through an event mesh. A trace is made up of the spans that are emitted at each point along the journey. An OpenTelemetry backend, such as Jaeger or DataDog, assembles these spans into a trace and provides a visual view of the entire lifespan of an event message. Trace context is the metadata that allows new spans to be created as children of another span in the same trace, or to link to other spans in the trace, regardless of where the other spans are generated. Context propagation is the mechanism by which that metadata is carried by event messages across service and process boundaries. Personally Identifiable Information (PII) Exposure—Distributed tracing captures metadata about message flow, including topic names, queue names, and message properties. This trace information is propagated through your event mesh and sent to observability backends. To minimize PII exposure, avoid including sensitive personal information in topic strings, queue names, user properties, or other message metadata that becomes part of the trace context. Context Propagation in the Event Broker Two types of context can be propagated in event messages: Span context— Solace Messaging API and event brokers can interpret and modify trace context within a message as the message is transported. Baggage (also known as correlation context)—Baggage is a set of key-value pairs that can be injected into and read from the context. Solace Messaging API s and event brokers never modify baggage; they only read it and include it in trace messages. Trace context and baggage are carried in the event message. If an event message is successfully traced (that is, it generates and enqueues a span), the event broker updates the event message's trace context with the context from the span. If, at any point after receiving an event message but before generating a span, the event broker encounters a condition where it cannot trace the event message (for example, the telemetry queue is full), the broker passes the trace context unchanged in the event message to the next hop in the event mesh. To handle event messages and generate spans appropriately, the event broker does the following when it receives an event message: Performs basic checks (such as verifying that the message's receive flow is valid, the message is the next expected message on that flow, the message complies with any topic access controls). If any of these tests fail, the message is discarded without tracing. Determines whether the event message is to be traced. If the message is to be traced, it sets the sampled flag in the message's trace context. Otherwise, it leaves the sampled flag unchanged. Processes the event message. Uses the event message's trace context to create a child span. Updates the event message's trace context at the same time the event message and trace message are persisted. The event message's trace context isn't updated sooner so that, if the event broker encounters a condition where it cannot trace the event message (for example, the telemetry queue is full), it doesn't generate a trace message—it passes the received trace context unchanged in the event message to the next hop in the event mesh. The event broker supports distributed tracing context propagation over HTTP and AMQP. Applications that use HTTP or AMQP to publish or consume messages from an event broker can propagate context information according to W3C standards. For more information, see: HTTP— https://www.w3.org/TR/trace-context/ AMQP— https://w3c.github.io/trace-context-amqp/ Context Propagation with Solace Messaging API s Context propagation with Solace Messaging API s is supported by the Solace OpenTelemetry Integration API Libraries. These libraries allow you to inject context into or extract context from SAP event messages. You deploy the Solace OpenTelemetry Integration API Libraries alongside the Solace Messaging API for the programming language you are using to develop your application. These libraries have dependencies on the OpenTelemetry API (which must also be deployed with your application). The Solace OpenTelemetry Integration API Libraries support W3C propagators only. Visualizing Trace Information in the Backend Tracing helps you understand the path an event message takes as it travels through distributed systems. A trace is made of one or more spans, with the first span representing the root span. Every root span represents a request from start to finish. As a request moves through a distributed system, it usually generates multiple spans. If a new span is generated by a request it is considered a child span, and the span that generated it is its parent. Child spans provide additional context of the steps that occur during a request. OpenTelemetry receivers collect different types of spans, including spans from the OpenTelemetry APIs and the event broker. The OpenTelemetry Collector collects, processes and exports these spans to a backend, where they are assembled into waterfall visualizations of end-to-end traces. These visualizations show the relationship between a root span and its child spans, and can help you improve and debug your distributed applications. The diagram below shows how spans generated at different points in a distributed system are assembled into an end-to-end trace in an OpenTelemetry backend: For a detailed explanation of how spans are collected into a trace, see OpenTelemetry Traces. Release Notes, and Support Event Broker Features / Distributed Tracing / Context Propagation for Distributed Tracing On this page Context Propagation in the Event Broker Context Propagation with Solace Messaging APIs Visualizing Trace Information in the Backend Context Propagation for Distributed Tracing As discussed in the overview, distributed tracing tracks an event all the way from the publishing application, between event brokers, and to the receiving application. A distributed trace, or simply a trace, is a record of the path taken by an event message as it propagates through an event mesh. A trace is made up of the spans that are emitted at each point along the journey. An OpenTelemetry backend, such as Jaeger or DataDog, assembles these spans into a trace and provides a visual view of the entire lifespan of an event message. Trace context is the metadata that allows new spans to be created as children of another span in the same trace, or to link to other spans in the trace, regardless of where the other spans are generated. Context propagation is the mechanism by which that metadata is carried by event messages across service and process boundaries. Personally Identifiable Information (PII) Exposure—Distributed tracing captures metadata about message flow, including topic names, queue names, and message properties. This trace information is propagated through your event mesh and sent to observability backends. To minimize PII exposure, avoid including sensitive personal information in topic strings, queue names, user properties, or other message metadata that becomes part of the trace context. Let's take a look at an example to see how context propagation works. The diagram below shows a simple architecture that includes one event broker and two clients (one that publishes messages, and one that consumes messages). Each process that handles the event message emits a span. These spans are consumed by a receiver, and then assembled together by an OpenTelemetry backend system to form a trace.Let's examine each step in this simplified example: Service A uses telemetry instrumentation to generate context information that can be added to messages. Service A prepares an event message using a Solace Messaging API, and injects trace context into that message using a OpenTelemetry Integration API Library. Service A might also separately generate OpenTelemetry spans with that same context using an OpenTelemetry API. The Solace Messaging API sends the message to the event broker. The event broker receives the message. If the broker has trace filter subscriptions that match the message's topic, it generates one or more spans. The spans are added to one or more trace messages, and the event broker sends the trace message to the telemetry queue. If the trace message is successfully enqueued, the event broker updates the event message's trace context with the context of the span. This starts a send span, which is the trace context sent with the message to the next hop (which could be another event broker or a consuming application). In this example, the next hop is the consuming application, Service B. Service B consumes the event message via Solace Messaging API, and extracts the trace context using a OpenTelemetry Integration API Library. It then uses this trace context to establish the parent span for the first span that is created upon receive. For send spans, the event broker records the time it sent the message to Service B as the start time for the send span. When the message is consumed by Service B, an acknowledgment is sent to the event broker. The event broker receives the acknowledgment, ends the send span, and records the outcome of the delivery attempt to Service B. At any time, the Solace OpenTelemetry Receiver can connect to the event broker to receive the Solace trace messages and convert them to standard OpenTelemetry spans. Other receiver modules receive the telemetry spans created by the producer and consumer applications. The OpenTelemetry Collector processes all the spans, which can then be correlated by a backend monitoring tool. At each hop in an event mesh, the event message is updated with the latest context information. This extends the overall trace as the event message moves through the mesh. Delivery of a send span is not guaranteed. Context Propagation in the Event Broker Two types of context can be propagated in event messages: Span context— Solace Messaging API and event brokers can interpret and modify trace context within a message as the message is transported. Baggage (also known as correlation context)—Baggage is a set of key-value pairs that can be injected into and read from the context. Solace Messaging API s and event brokers never modify baggage; they only read it and include it in trace messages. Trace context and baggage are carried in the event message. If an event message is successfully traced (that is, it generates and enqueues a span), the event broker updates the event message's trace context with the context from the span. If, at any point after receiving an event message but before generating a span, the event broker encounters a condition where it cannot trace the event message (for example, the telemetry queue is full), the broker passes the trace context unchanged in the event message to the next hop in the event mesh. To handle event messages and generate spans appropriately, the event broker does the following when it receives an event message: Performs basic checks (such as verifying that the message's receive flow is valid, the message is the next expected message on that flow, the message complies with any topic access controls). If any of these tests fail, the message is discarded without tracing. Determines whether the event message is to be traced. If the message is to be traced, it sets the sampled flag in the message's trace context. Otherwise, it leaves the sampled flag unchanged. Processes the event message. Uses the event message's trace context to create a child span. Updates the event message's trace context at the same time the event message and trace message are persisted. The event message's trace context isn't updated sooner so that, if the event broker encounters a condition where it cannot trace the event message (for example, the telemetry queue is full), it doesn't generate a trace message—it passes the received trace context unchanged in the event message to the next hop in the event mesh. The event broker supports distributed tracing context propagation over HTTP and AMQP. Applications that use HTTP or AMQP to publish or consume messages from an event broker can propagate context information according to W3C standards. For more information, see: HTTP— https://www.w3.org/TR/trace-context/ AMQP— https://w3c.github.io/trace-context-amqp/ Context Propagation with Solace Messaging API s Context propagation with Solace Messaging API s is supported by the OpenTelemetry Integration API Libraries. These libraries allow you to inject context into or extract context from Solace event messages. You deploy the OpenTelemetry Integration API Libraries alongside the Solace Messaging API for the programming language you are using to develop your application. These libraries have dependencies on the OpenTelemetry API (which must also be deployed with your application). The OpenTelemetry Integration API Libraries support W3C propagators only. See the following links for information about specific libraries, including where you can get them: For OpenTelemetry Integration API for the Solace Messaging API for Java, see Context Propagation for Distributed Tracing in the Solace Java API. For OpenTelemetry Integration API for the Solace Messaging API for JMS, see Instrumenting JMS and Jakarta for Distributed Tracing. For OpenTelemetry Integration API for the Solace Messaging API for JCSMP, see Instrumenting JCSMP for Distributed Tracing. For OpenTelemetry Integration API for the Solace Messaging API for .NET, see Instrumenting .NET for Distributed Tracing. For OpenTelemetry Integration API for the Solace Messaging API for JavaScript and Solace Messaging API for Node.js, see Instrumenting Node.js for Distributed Tracing. For OpenTelemetry Integration API for the Solace Messaging API for Go, see Instrumenting Go for Distributed Tracing. For OpenTelemetry Integration API for the Solace Messaging API for Python, see Instrumenting Python for Distributed Tracing. Visualizing Trace Information in the Backend Tracing helps you understand the path an event message takes as it travels through distributed systems. A trace is made of one or more spans, with the first span representing the root span. Every root span represents a request from start to finish. As a request moves through a distributed system, it usually generates multiple spans. If a new span is generated by a request it is considered a child span, and the span that generated it is its parent. Child spans provide additional context of the steps that occur during a request. OpenTelemetry receivers collect different types of spans, including spans from the OpenTelemetry APIs and the event broker. The OpenTelemetry Collector collects, processes and exports these spans to a backend, where they are assembled into waterfall visualizations of end-to-end traces. These visualizations show the relationship between a root span and its child spans, and can help you improve and debug your distributed applications. The diagram below shows how spans generated at different points in a distributed system are assembled into an end-to-end trace in an OpenTelemetry backend:For a detailed explanation of how spans are collected into a trace, see OpenTelemetry Traces. documentation issue On this page Context Propagation Example Traces Logs Metrics Custom Context Propagation Security best practices External services Baggage Support in Language SDKs Specification Docs Concepts Context propagation Context propagation Learn about the concept that enables Distributed Tracing. With context propagation, signals ( traces, metrics, and logs) can be correlated with each other, regardless of where they are generated. Although not limited to tracing, context propagation allows traces to build causal information about a system across services that are arbitrarily distributed across process and network boundaries. To understand context propagation, you need to understand two separate concepts: context and propagation. Context Context is an object that contains the information for the sending and receiving service, or execution unit, to correlate one signal with another. When Service A calls Service B, Service A includes a trace ID and a span ID as part of the context. Service B uses these values to create a new span that belongs to the same trace, setting the span from Service A as its parent. This makes it possible to track the full flow of a request across service boundaries. Propagation Propagation is the mechanism that moves context between services and processes. It serializes or deserializes the context object and provides the relevant information to be propagated from one service to another. Propagation is usually handled by instrumentation libraries and is transparent to the user. In the event that you need to manually propagate context, you can use the Propagators API. OpenTelemetry maintains several official propagators. The default propagator uses the headers specified by the W3C TraceContext specification. Example A service called Frontend that provides different HTTP endpoints such as POST /cart/add and GET /checkout/ reaches out to a downstream service Product Catalog via an HTTP endpoint GET /product to receive details on products that a user wants to add to the cart or that are part of the checkout. To understand activities in the Product Catalog service within the context of requests coming from Frontend , the context (here: Trace ID and Span ID as “Parent ID”) is propagated using the traceparent header as it is defined in the W3C TraceContext specification. This means the IDs are embedded in the fields of the header: For example: Traces As mentioned, context propagation allows traces to build causal information across services. In this example, the two calls to the HTTP endpoint GET /product of service Product Catalog can be correlated with their upstream calls in service Frontend by extracting the remote context from the traceparent header and injecting it into the local context to set the Trace ID and Parent ID. With that, it is possible in a backend like Jaeger to see two requests as spans of one trace. Logs OpenTelemetry SDKs are able to automatically correlate logs with traces. This means they can inject context (Trace ID, Span ID) into a log record. This not only enables you to see logs in the context of the trace and span they belong to, but it also enables you to see logs that belong together across service or execution unit boundaries. Metrics In the case of metrics, context propagation enables you to aggregate measurements in that context. For example, instead of only looking at the response time of all the GET /product requests, you can also get metrics for combinations of POST /cart/add > GET /product and GET /checkout < GET /product . Custom Context Propagation For most use cases, you will find instrumentation libraries or native library instrumentation that handle the context propagation for you. In some cases no such support is available and you want to create that support for yourself. To do so you need to leverage the previously mentioned Propagators API: On the side of the sender, the context is injected into the carrier, for example, into the headers of an HTTP request. In other cases, you need to find a place that can store metadata for your request. On the receiving side, the context is extracted from the carrier. Again, in the case of HTTP, this is retrieved from the headers. In other cases, you pick the place you chose on the sending side to store the context. Note that it is possible to propagate context in protocols that do not have a dedicated field for metadata, but you have to make sure that on the receiving side they are extracted and removed before the data is processed, otherwise you may create undefined behavior. For the following languages a step-by-step tutorial exists for custom context propagation: Erlang JavaScript PHP Python Security best practices Propagation involves sending and receiving data across service boundaries, which can have security implications. External services When your service interacts with external services (services you do not own or trust), consider the following: Incoming context: Be cautious when accepting context from external sources. Malicious actors could send forged trace headers to manipulate your tracing data or potentially exploit vulnerabilities in context parsing. You might want to ignore or sanitize incoming context from untrusted sources. Outgoing context: Be mindful of what you propagate to external services. Internal trace IDs, span IDs, or baggage items might reveal sensitive information about your internal architecture or business logic. You may want to configure your propagators to not send context to external or public-facing endpoints. Baggage Baggage allows you to propagate arbitrary key-value pairs. Since this data is propagated across service boundaries, avoid putting sensitive information (like user credentials, API keys, or PII) in baggage, as it might be logged or sent to untrusted downstream services. Support in Language SDKs For the individual language-specific implementations of the OpenTelemetry API & SDK, you will find details on the support of context propagation in the respective documentation pages: C++ .NET Erlang Go Java JavaScript PHP Python Ruby Rust Swift Help wanted For languages .NET, Rust, and Swift, the language-specific documentation for context propagation is missing. If you know any of those languages and are interested to help, learn how you can contribute! Specification To learn more about context propagation, see the Context specification. Feedback Was this page helpful? Yes No Thank you. Your feedback is appreciated! Please let us know how we can improve this page. Your feedback is appreciated! Source: Continuous profiling in production: A real-world example to measure ...URL: https://grafana.com/blog/continuous-profiling-in-production-a-real-world-example-to-measure-benefits-and-costs/What’s new: Grafana 13 release, the latest in AI, OSS project updates, and more from GrafanaCON2026 Learn moreDownloadsContact Us Pricing Docs AI/SearchSign inSign upSite Search Ask AI Continuous profiling in production: A real-world example to measure benefits and costs Jake Kramer • 2026-02-09 • 8 minContinuous profiling offers deep visibility into production environments, revealing exactly how applications consume CPU and memory. It’s the go-to observability practice for directly connecting system behavior and performance to specific lines of code. But when teams consider deploying continuous profiling more broadly, a common question comes up: what’s the overhead? Is it safe to run continuous profiling on my production services 24/7, or does the cost outweigh the benefits? As a Senior Software Engineer for Pyroscope, the open source continuous profiling database, I hope to answer that question in this post. I’ll cover why profiling is so valuable, what “cost” really means in terms of resource usage and engineering hours, and share our own experience running continuous profiling at scale here at Grafana Labs. Why use continuous profiling in production? Before talking about cost, let’s explore the benefits of deploying continuous profiling in the first place. Continuous profiling gives you a time series of where your applications spend resources, down to the line of code. That’s a different level of insight than simply knowing, for example, that a service is using 80% CPU. With continuous profiling, you see which functions and which requests are responsible for that 80%. That, in turn, lets you pinpoint root causes without needing to reproduce the issue in staging or add new instrumentation. You can identify the highest impact fixes (or roll back with confidence), which shortens MTTR. In a GrafanaCON 2025 talk, we explained that continuous profiling uniquely improves both sides of the ROI equation: Revenue/reliability: Higher throughput, lower latency, and better user experience. Cost: Less resource waste, lower infrastructure spend, and faster incident detection and resolution. So the real question isn’t whether continuous profiling has overhead (it does), but whether that overhead is small and predictable enough that the optimization wins outweigh the cost. The cost equation with continuous profiling When people say “overhead” in the context of continuous profiling, they usually mean the runtime impact on the application: CPU, memory, and latency. But there are also backend costs, such as storing and querying profiles, as well as human costs, meaning your team’s time. Let’s dig into some of these, starting with what happens on the node. CPU and latency overhead: within a few percentage points A lot of the hesitation around continuous profiling and overhead stems from old-school profilers that instrumented every call, wrote huge traces, or periodically halted application execution. Pyroscope’s model is different: it relies on sampling profilers, which take a stack trace 100 times per second (100 Hz). That’s enough for a good statistical picture without touching every function call. In a recent deep-dive post on native-code profiling with eBPF, written by Grafana Labs Staff Software Engineer Oleg Kozliuk, we explored real workloads that were benchmarked with and without profiling. The impact on throughput and latency was within a few percentage points in both cases. The setup used eBPF via Alloy and Grafana Cloud Profiles, the hosted continuous profiling tool powered by Pyroscope, but the mechanics were the same: periodic stack sampling plus lightweight metadata. In practice, CPU and latency overhead for typical configurations lands in the low single-digit percentages, often small enough that you need synthetic benchmarks to even see it. Memory overhead: tens of megabytes, not gigabytes Sampling profilers are designed to have very low memory overhead, usually less than 50 MB per pod. Here’s what’s happening under the hood: Stack traces are sampled at ~100 Hz. Allocation and lock events are sampled. Samples are stored in memory. Every 15 seconds (by default), profiles are flushed to the backend. The main memory cost is an in-process buffer, not giant in-memory flame graphs. In practice at Grafana Labs, the overhead is often so small that the profiler's memory usage is hard to see in the profile relative to the application's memory usage. Under the hood: where does that overhead come from? To understand why overhead is low (and what can make it higher), it helps to look at the profiler’s control loop.Sampling loop At the heart of most continuous profilers: A timer fires at a fixed rate (e.g., 100 Hz). The profiler captures the current call stack (and maybe some metadata). The stack trace becomes a sample. This step is intentionally lightweight: It captures stack traces, not whole heaps or object graphs. It avoids blocking your critical path as much as possible.In-process aggregation and buffering Instead of streaming each sample individually, Pyroscope clients: Aggregate samples into compact structures. Buffer them in memory. Flush them every 15 seconds (by default) to the backend. Multiple threads are used for uploads, but the guiding principle of Pyroscope clients is to never cause the user application to crash: fail by dropping profiles, not by hurting your application.Language and profiler differences Overhead also depends on how you collect data: Go: Uses Go’s built-in sampling profiler (and sometimes eBPF). Note there are push (via SDK) and pull (via Alloy’s  pyroscope.scrape  component) options for sending profiles to Pyroscope. Java: Uses AsyncProfiler or Java Flight Recorder via the Pyroscope Java agent (in the JVM process), or auto-instrumentation using Alloy's  pyroscope.java  component externally attaching to a running JVM using AsyncProfiler. .NET, Python, Ruby, Node.js, Rust: Use runtime profilers or eBPF-based approaches depending on platform. eBPF/system-wide: Pyroscope’s eBPF integration (via Alloy and  pyroscope.ebpf ) hooks perf events at the kernel level to sample stacks across processes without changing application code. Each has different characteristics, but they share the same philosophy: sampling, not deep instrumentation. What actually drives the cost? Even within a single language, overhead varies. The main profiling knobs are: Sampling rate Higher frequency (e.g., 200 Hz) → better resolution, more CPU overhead. Lower frequency (e.g., 50 Hz) → less overhead, coarser view. The default 100 Hz works well for most teams. If you’re extremely latency-sensitive, you can reduce rates for specific services. Profile types More profile types = more work: CPU profiles are the cheapest and most common. Allocation/heap profiles add bookkeeping. Mutex/block/goroutine profiles add lock and scheduling data. At Grafana Labs, we often run with multiple profile types enabled by default: CPU, allocations, goroutines, mutex, block, and still see per-pod memory usage stay below ~50 MB. If you’re cost-sensitive: Start with CPU-only. Turn on additional profile types in targeted experiments (a subset of services or a specific namespace). Workload characteristics Some workloads are trickier to profile: Very short-lived tasks (sub-millisecond) can be hard to sample. High-churn workloads (many short connections/processes) create more sampling and aggregation work. Highly optimized native code (e.g., vectorized DB kernels) may be more sensitive to any extra overhead. The good news is you can measure this in your own environment with a simple method: run a representative load test with profiling off vs. on and compare latency and throughput. Backend and storage costs: why the server also matters So far we’ve focused on application overhead, but continuous profiling also has a database backend like Pyroscope or Grafana Cloud Profiles. Here the cost is storage and query performance as you scale. Pyroscope shares architecture patterns with systems like Loki, Mimir, and Tempo (sharded, horizontally scalable, block-based storage), so it inherits the same profiling, compaction, and query-optimization work. For users, that means: Lower storage cost per unit of profiling data. Faster queries over long time ranges. Predictable performance as you add services and tenants. How to do your own “overhead reality check” with Pyroscope Pick a canary + steady loadChoose a representative, non-critical service. Put it under stable traffic (production during a quiet window or fixed-load test). Enable your profiler just thereEnable the profiler with CPU-only and default sampling (e.g., 100 Hz). Optionally, tag it (e.g.,  profiling=canary ) so you can filter later. Watch your normal metricsCompare profiled vs. unprofiled pods of the same service: CPU per pod p95/p99 latency Error rate This gives you a first-pass “profiling ≈ X% CPU / Y ms” answer. Use Pyroscope to see profiler costIn Pyroscope: Filter to the canary. Open a CPU flame graph. Search for profiler frames ( pprof ,  async-profiler ,  jfr , etc.). The percentage of samples in those frames ≈ profiler overhead (e.g., 2% of samples ≈ ~2% CPU). When someone asks, “Is this cost-effective in production?” you won’t be guessing — you’ll have numbers from your own environment, measured by Pyroscope. Bringing it all together If you’ve had bad experiences with heavy profilers, skepticism is reasonable. But modern sampling profilers, in combination with Pyroscope, tell a different story: Memory overhead is typically <50 MB per pod, even with multiple profile types enabled. CPU and latency overhead in realistic benchmarks sits in the low single-digit percentage range. In other words, you’re trading a small, predictable overhead for the ability to: Catch performance regressions before they hit customers. Make expensive infrastructure actually earn its keep. Answer “where is the CPU going?” in minutes instead of days. That’s a tradeoff we’re happy to make across all of our own production workloads at Grafana Labs, and one that more and more teams are adopting as continuous profiling moves from nice-to-have to a standard observability pillar. To learn more, please check out our Pyroscope documentation or reach out in the #pyroscope channel in our Grafana Labs Community Slack. Sign in How to Compare OpenTelemetry Backend Options for Cost and Performance A practical framework for evaluating OpenTelemetry backend options based on cost, query performance, scalability, and operational overhead. @nawazdhandala • Feb 06, 2026 • Reading time 7 min read OpenTelemetry Backend Comparison Cost Analysis Performance Observability Storage On this page The Comparison Framework Self-Hosted Open-Source Options Managed SaaS Options Building a Cost Model Performance Benchmarking Decision Matrix Migration Considerations Wrapping UpPicking an OpenTelemetry backend is one of those decisions that is easy to get wrong and expensive to change later. The ecosystem has grown rapidly, and you can now choose from managed SaaS platforms, open-source self-hosted solutions, and hybrid approaches. Each option has different cost characteristics, performance profiles, and operational requirements. This guide provides a framework for comparing backend options based on the factors that actually matter in production: cost at your scale, query performance for your use cases, operational overhead, and long-term flexibility. The Comparison Framework Before diving into specific backends, you need to define what you are comparing. The four dimensions that matter most are ingestion cost, storage cost, query performance, and operational cost. Most teams fixate on the first two and ignore the last two, which is a mistake. A free open-source backend that requires two full-time engineers to operate is not actually cheaper than a managed service. Backend Evaluation Ingestion Cost Storage Cost Query Performance Operational Cost Per-span/sample price Network transfer costs Retention period needs Compression ratio Query latency P50/P99 Concurrent query capacity Infrastructure to manage Team expertise required Self-Hosted Open-Source Options The open-source landscape for OpenTelemetry backends is rich. Here are the most common options with their cost and performance characteristics. Jaeger with Elasticsearch is the classic trace backend. Elasticsearch handles full-text search well, which makes trace queries by attribute fast. But Elasticsearch is memory-hungry and needs careful tuning. At moderate scale (around 50,000 spans per second), you are looking at a cluster of 6 to 12 nodes with 64GB RAM each. Storage costs depend on retention, but expect roughly 500 bytes per span after indexing. Jaeger with Cassandra trades query flexibility for write throughput. Cassandra handles high write volumes better than Elasticsearch, but trace search is more limited. You lose the ability to search by arbitrary span attributes unless you build secondary indexes. Cost-wise, Cassandra uses less memory per node but needs more nodes for the same query performance. ClickHouse has become a popular choice for all three signal types. It uses columnar storage with aggressive compression, which means your storage costs can be 5 to 10 times lower than Elasticsearch for the same data. Query performance on aggregations is excellent, but point queries (finding a single trace by ID) are slower than in a purpose-built system. A ClickHouse cluster handling 100,000 spans per second typically needs 3 to 6 nodes with NVMe storage. Prometheus with Thanos or Cortex covers the metrics side. Raw Prometheus is free but limited in retention and scalability. Adding Thanos pushes historical data to object storage (S3 or GCS), which costs roughly $0.02 per GB per month. Cortex adds multi-tenancy but has higher operational complexity. Here is a rough cost comparison for self-hosted options processing 100,000 spans per second with 30-day retention. Managed SaaS Options Managed backends shift the operational cost to a vendor in exchange for a per-unit price that is typically higher than self-hosting at scale. The crossover point where self-hosting becomes cheaper varies, but it is usually around 50,000 to 100,000 spans per second for traces and 500,000 active series for metrics. The main advantage of managed services is not just operations. It is the features that come built in: alerting, dashboards, anomaly detection, and correlation between signals. Building these yourself on top of open-source backends takes significant engineering effort. When evaluating managed services, ask for pricing at your expected scale, not just the getting-started tier. Many vendors offer attractive entry pricing that scales poorly. Request a quote for your actual volume: the number of spans per second, active metric series, log volume in GB per day, and retention requirements. Building a Cost Model To compare options fairly, build a cost model that accounts for all expenses. Here is a template. The operational cost is where most comparisons fall apart. A team of two engineers spending 20% of their time operating a self-hosted backend at $200K fully-loaded salary each costs $6,667 per month. That needs to be added to the infrastructure cost for a fair comparison. Performance Benchmarking Cost only tells half the story. You also need to benchmark query performance for your actual query patterns. Here are the queries you should test. Run these benchmarks at your expected data volume, not on an empty database. Backend performance often degrades as data accumulates, so load at least a week of realistic data before benchmarking. Decision Matrix Here is a practical decision matrix that maps common scenarios to recommended approaches. For teams with fewer than 5 engineers and moderate scale (under 50K spans per second), a managed SaaS backend is usually the right choice. The operational savings outweigh the higher per-unit cost. For teams with a dedicated platform or SRE team and high scale (over 100K spans per second), self-hosted ClickHouse or a Jaeger plus Cassandra combination gives you the best cost efficiency. You have the team to operate it and the scale to justify the operational investment. For teams in between, a hybrid approach works well. Use a managed service for traces and logs where query UX matters, and self-hosted Prometheus with Thanos for metrics where the tooling is mature and the operational burden is lower. < 50K spans/sec < 5 engineers 5+ engineers Yes No Scale? Team size? Platform team? Managed SaaS Hybrid approach Self-hosted ClickHouse or Jaeger+Cassandra Managed SaaS with volume discount Migration Considerations Whichever backend you choose, plan for the possibility of switching later. The OpenTelemetry Collector is your best friend here. By routing all telemetry through a collector, your applications are decoupled from the backend. Changing backends becomes a collector configuration change, not an application code change. During migration, run both backends in parallel using the multi-exporter pattern. Send data to both the old and new backends for at least two weeks. Compare query results, verify that dashboards and alerts produce the same results, and measure the performance difference with real data. Wrapping Up There is no universally best OpenTelemetry backend. The right choice depends on your scale, your team, and your priorities. Build a real cost model that includes operational overhead, benchmark with realistic data and query patterns, and use the collector to keep your options open. The most expensive mistake is not picking the wrong backend. It is picking one that you cannot change when your needs evolve. Sign in How to Implement Continuous Profiling with OpenTelemetry Learn how to set up continuous profiling with OpenTelemetry for production performance analysis. @nawazdhandala • Jan 07, 2026 • Reading time 27 min read OpenTelemetry Observability Profiling Performance CPU Memory On this page What is Continuous Profiling? Architecture Overview Setting Up the Environment Implementing CPU Profiling Implementing Memory Profiling Trace Correlation with Profiles Exporting Profiles to OpenTelemetry Collector OpenTelemetry Collector Configuration Complete Integration Example Production Best Practices Visualization and Analysis Troubleshooting Common Issues Conclusion Further ReadingContinuous profiling is a powerful observability technique that provides deep insights into application performance by continuously collecting profiling data in production environments. When combined with OpenTelemetry, you gain the ability to correlate profiling data with traces, metrics, and logs, creating a comprehensive view of your application's behavior. In this guide, we will explore how to implement continuous profiling with OpenTelemetry, covering CPU and memory profiling, trace correlation, and production best practices. What is Continuous Profiling? Continuous profiling is the practice of collecting profiling data from applications running in production on an ongoing basis, rather than only during development or troubleshooting sessions. This approach provides: Always-on visibility: Capture performance issues as they happen Historical analysis: Compare performance across deployments Resource optimization: Identify inefficient code paths and memory leaks Cost reduction: Optimize resource usage based on actual production behavior Architecture Overview The following diagram illustrates how continuous profiling integrates with OpenTelemetry: Analysis & Visualization Backend Storage Data Collection Application Runtime Correlation Flame Graph UI Trace UI Performance Dashboard Profile Database Trace Database Metrics Database OpenTelemetry Collector Profile Processor Application Code Profiling Agent OpenTelemetry SDK Setting Up the Environment Before we begin implementing continuous profiling, ensure you have the necessary dependencies installed. This allows you to see exactly which code paths are causing performance issues for specific requests. The following diagram shows how trace and profile correlation works: Backend Storage OTel Collector Profiler OTel Tracer Application Client Backend Storage OTel Collector Profiler OTel Tracer Application Client Profile collection with trace_id annotation Profile and trace linked by trace_id for analysis HTTP Request Start Span (trace_id, span_id) Attach trace context Execute business logic Collect CPU/Memory samples End Span Send Trace Send Profile (with trace_id) Store with correlation The following code implements trace-correlated profiling by embedding trace context into profile labels: Exporting Profiles to OpenTelemetry Collector To integrate with the OpenTelemetry ecosystem, we need to export profiles in a format that the collector can process. By implementing the patterns and practices covered in this guide, you can: Collect CPU and memory profiles continuously with minimal overhead Correlate profiles with distributed traces for root cause analysis Export profiles to OpenTelemetry-compatible backends Apply production-grade resource limits and security measures Visualize and analyze performance data effectively The key to successful continuous profiling is balance: collecting enough data to be useful while minimizing impact on production performance. Start with conservative settings and adjust based on your specific requirements and system characteristics.
